{{2019–20 Hong Kong protests sidebar}}
{{Chinese democracy movement}}

The ongoing '''2019–20 Hong Kong protests''', also known as '''Anti-Extradition Law Amendment Bill Movement''', were triggered by the introduction of the [[2019 Hong Kong extradition bill|Fugitive Offenders amendment bill]] by the [[Hong Kong government]]. The now aborted bill would have allowed [[extradition]] to jurisdictions with which Hong Kong did not have extradition agreements, including [[mainland China]] and [[Taiwan]]. This led to concerns that Hong Kong residents and visitors would be exposed to the [[Law of the People's Republic of China|legal system of mainland China]], thereby undermining [[One country, two systems|Hong Kong's autonomy]] and infringing civil liberties. It set off a chain of protest actions that began with a sit-in at the government headquarters on 15 March 2019, a demonstration attended by hundreds of thousands on 9&nbsp;June 2019, followed by a gathering outside the [[Legislative Council Complex]] to stall the bill's second reading on 12&nbsp;June which [[612 Incident|escalated into violence]] that caught the world's attention.

On 16 June, just one day after the Hong Kong government suspended the bill, an even bigger protest took place to push for its complete withdrawal and in reaction to the perceived excessive use of force by the police on 12&nbsp;June. As the protests progressed, citizens laid out five key demands, namely the withdrawal of the bill, an investigation into [[Police misconduct allegations during the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests|alleged police brutality and misconduct]], the release of all the arrested, a retraction of the official characterisation of the protests as "riots", and the resignation of [[Carrie Lam]] as [[Chief Executive of Hong Kong|chief executive]] along with the introduction of [[universal suffrage]] in the territory. Police inaction when suspected [[Triad (organized crime)|triad]] members [[2019 Yuen Long attack|assaulted protesters and commuters in Yuen Long]] on 21&nbsp;July and the [[2019 Prince Edward station attack|police storming of Prince Edward station]] on 31&nbsp;August further escalated the protests.

Lam withdrew the bill on 4&nbsp;September, but refused to concede the other four demands. Exactly one month later, she invoked the [[Emergency Regulations Ordinance|emergency powers]] to implement an [[anti-mask law]], to counterproductive effect. Confrontations escalated and intensified – police brutality and misconduct allegations increased, while some protesters started using [[petrol bomb]]s and vandalising pro-Beijing establishments and symbols representing the government. Rifts within society widened and activists from both sides assaulted each other. The [[storming of the Legislative Council]] in July 2019, the deaths of [[Death of Chow Tsz-lok|Chow Tsz-lok]] and [[Death of Luo Changqing|Luo Changqing]], the shooting of an unarmed protester, and the sieges of two universities in November 2019 were landmark events.

After the [[Chinese University of Hong Kong conflict|conflict in the Chinese University]] and [[siege of the Hong Kong Polytechnic University|siege of the Polytechnic University]], the unprecedented landslide victory of the [[pro-democracy camp]] in the [[2019 Hong Kong local elections|District Council election]] in November and the [[COVID-19 pandemic]] in early 2020 brought a little respite. Tensions mounted again in May 2020 after Beijing's [[National People's Congress decision on Hong Kong national security legislation|decision to promulgate a national security bill]] for Hong Kong before September. This was seen by many as a threat to fundamental political freedoms and civil liberties ostensibly enshrined in the [[Hong Kong Basic Law]], and prompted the international community to re-evaluate their policies towards Hong Kong, which they deemed was no longer autonomous.

The approval ratings of the government and the police plunged to the lowest point since the [[handover of Hong Kong|1997 handover]]; the [[Central People's Government]] alleged that foreign powers were instigating the conflict, although the protests have been largely described as "leaderless". The United States passed the [[Hong Kong Human Rights and Democracy Act]] on 27&nbsp;November 2019 to support the protest movement. The [[tactics and methods surrounding the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests|tactics and methods used]] have inspired other protests in 2019.

== Background ==
{{main|Causes of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests}}

===Direct cause===
The [[Fugitive Offenders and Mutual Legal Assistance in Criminal Matters Legislation (Amendment) Bill 2019]] was first proposed by the [[government of Hong Kong]] in February 2019 in response to the 2018 [[murder of Poon Hiu-wing]] by her boyfriend Chan Tong-kai in [[Taiwan]], where the two Hong Kong residents were visiting as tourists. As there is no extradition treaty with Taiwan (because the [[government of China]] does not recognise its [[Political status of Taiwan|sovereignty]]), the Hong Kong government proposed an amendment to the Fugitive Offenders Ordinance ({{cite Hong Kong ordinance|503}}) and Mutual Legal Assistance in Criminal Matters Ordinance ({{cite Hong Kong ordinance|525}}) to establish a mechanism for case-by-case transfers of fugitives, on the order of the chief executive, to any jurisdiction with which the city lacks a formal extradition treaty.<ref name="scmp_3004067" />

The inclusion of [[mainland China]] in the amendment is of concern to Hong Kong society: Citizens, academics and the legal profession fear the removal of the separation of the region's jurisdiction from the legal system administered by the [[Communist Party of China|Communist Party]] would erode the "[[one country, two systems]]" principle in practice since the 1997 handover; furthermore, Hong Kong citizens lack confidence in China's judiciary system and human rights protection due to its history of suppressing political dissent.<ref name="theconversation118753" /> Opponents of the bill urged the Hong Kong government to explore other mechanisms, such as an extradition arrangement solely with Taiwan, and to [[Sunset provision|sunset]] the arrangement immediately after the surrender of the suspect.<ref name="scmp_3004067" /><ref name="scmp_3006463" />

===Underlying causes===
The 2019–20 Hong Kong protests came four and a half years after the [[Umbrella Revolution]] of 2014 which begun after the [[2014 NPCSC Decision on Hong Kong|decision of the Standing Committee of the National People's Congress]] (NPCSC) regarding [[2014 Hong Kong electoral reform|proposed reforms to the Hong Kong electoral system]], which were largely seen as restrictive. However, the movement ended in failure as the government offered no concessions.<ref name="bbc_48655474" /> Since then, [[democratic development in Hong Kong|democratic development]] has stalled: only half of the seats in the [[Legislative Council of Hong Kong|Legislative Council]] remain directly elected, and the chief executive of Hong Kong continues to be elected by the small-circle [[Election Committee]]. The [[2017 imprisonment of Hong Kong democracy activists]] further dashed the city's hope of meaningful political reform.<ref name="time_5661211" /> Citizens began to fear the loss of the "high degree of autonomy" as provided for in the [[Hong Kong Basic Law]], as the government of the People's Republic of China appeared to be increasingly and overtly interfering with Hong Kong's affairs. Notably, the NPCSC saw fit to rule on the [[Hong Kong Legislative Council oath-taking controversy|disqualification of six lawmakers]]; fears over state-sanctioned rendition and [[extrajudicial detention]] were sparked by the [[Causeway Bay Books disappearances]].<ref name="bbc_48607723" /><ref name="time_5661211" /> The general trend of the loss of freedoms in Hong Kong is marked by its steady fall on the [[Economist Intelligence Unit]]'s [[Democracy Index]].<ref name="thestandard215668" /><ref name="jm3Nr" /> [[Xi Jinping]]'s accession to paramount leader of China in 2012 marked a more hardline authoritarian approach, most notably with the construction of [[Xinjiang re-education camps|concentration camps in Xinjiang]]. The spectre that Hong Kong may similarly be brought to heel became an important element in the protests.<ref name="caught fire" />

Anti-Mainland sentiment had begun to swell in the 2010s. The daily quota of 150 immigrants from China since 1997, and the massive flows of mainland visitors strained Hong Kong's public services and eroded local culture; mainlanders' arrogance drew the scorn of Hongkongers.<ref name="caught fire" /> The rise of [[Localism in Hong Kong|localism]] and the [[Hong Kong independence|pro-independence movement]] during the tenure of [[CY Leung]] as chief executive was marked by the high-profile campaign for the [[2016 New Territories East by-election]] by activist [[Edward Leung]].<ref name="scmp_3021518" /> As fewer and fewer young people in Hong Kong identified themselves as Chinese nationals, pollsters at the [[University of Hong Kong]] found that the younger respondents were, the more distrustful they were of the Chinese government.<ref name="bbc_48607723" /> Scandals and corruption in China shook people's confidence of the country's political systems; the [[Moral and National Education controversy]] in 2012 and the Express Rail Link project connecting Hong Kong with mainland cities and the subsequent co-location agreement [[Anti-Hong Kong Express Rail Link movement|proved highly controversial]]. Citizens saw these policies as Beijing's decision to strengthen its hold over Hong Kong. By 2019, almost no Hong Kong youth identified themselves as Chinese.<ref name="economist_20190826_almostnobody" />

The polite Umbrella Revolution had provided inspiration and brought about a political awakening to some,<ref name="bbc_48655474" /><ref name="uRWPA" /> but its failure and the subsequent split within the pro-democratic bloc prompted a re-evaluation of strategy and tactics. In the years that followed, the general consensus emerged that peaceful and polite protests were ineffective in advancing democratic development, and became an example of what not to do in further protests. Media noted that protests in 2019 were driven by a sense of desperation rather than the optimism in 2014.<ref name="guardian_20190702_moodhardens" /><ref name="cnn_20190702_protestsdesperation" /> The aims of the protests had evolved from withdrawing the bill, solidifying around achieving the level of freedom and liberties promised.<ref name="time_20190815_summerunrest" />

Economic factors were also cited as an underlying cause of anger among Hongkongers.<ref name="economist_20190822_unaffordablehousing" /><ref name="aljazeera_190801151538867" /><ref name="cnbc_20190412_averageprice" /> With powerful business cartels, Hong Kong suffers from [[income disparity]]<ref name="disparity" /> – the second highest [[Gini coefficient]] in the world in 2017<ref name="1AkHH" /> – and a shortage of affordable or public housing for the city's population.<ref name="scmp_3020789" /><ref name="scmp_1618427" /> Youth, who have been pre-eminent in the protests, are frustrated by low [[social mobility]] and the lack of job opportunities;<ref name="disparity" /><ref name="W9YM6" /> salaries for university graduates in 2018 were lower than those of 30 years previously.<ref name="disparity" /> Many protesters in Hong Kong were young and educated: Many of them were under the age of 30, and had received tertiary education.<ref name="rFgV9" />

== Objectives ==
Initially the protesters only demanded the withdrawal of the extradition bill. Following an escalation in the severity of policing tactics against demonstrators on 12&nbsp;June 2019, the protesters' objective was to achieve the following five demands (under the slogan "Five demands, not one less"):<ref name="focustw_20190627_urgeg20" />
* '''Complete withdrawal of the extradition bill from the legislative process''': Although the chief executive announced an indefinite suspension of the bill on 15&nbsp;June, its status of "pending resumption of second reading" in the [[Legislative Council of Hong Kong|Legislative Council]] meant that its reading could have been resumed quickly. It was formally withdrawn on 23&nbsp;October 2019.<ref name="reuters_20191023_officiallykilled" />
* '''Retraction of the "riot" characterisation''': The government originally characterised the 12&nbsp;June protest as "riots", it later amended the description to say there were "some" rioters, an assertion protesters still contest. The crime of "rioting" carries a maximum penalty of 10&nbsp;years in prison.
* '''Release and exoneration of arrested protesters''': Protesters consider their lawbreaking acts to be mostly motivated by a politically righteous cause; they also question the legitimacy of police arresting protesters at hospitals through access to their confidential medical data in breach of patient privacy.
* '''Establishment of an independent commission of inquiry into police conduct and use of force during the protests''': Civic groups felt that the level of violence used by the police against protesters and bystanders, arbitrary stop-and-search,<ref name="rfa_06112019114440" /> and officers' failure to observe Police General Orders pointed to a breakdown of accountability.<ref name="standnews_20190627" /> The absence of independence of the existing watchdog, the [[Independent Police Complaints Council]], is also an issue.<ref name="scmp_3030211" />
* '''Resignation of Carrie Lam and the implementation of universal suffrage for [[Hong Kong legislative elections|Legislative Council elections]] and for the election of the chief executive''':<ref name="nyt_20190708_moredemocracy" /> The chief executive is selected in a small-circle election, and 30 of the 70 legislative council seats are filled by representatives of institutionalised interest groups, forming the majority of the so-called [[Functional constituency (Hong Kong)|functional constituencies]], most of which have few electors.

==History==
===Early demonstrations===
{{Main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (March–June 2019)}}

[[File:Harcourt Road tear smoke view1 20190612.jpg|left|thumb|Police used [[tear gas]] to disperse protesters gathering outside the Legislative Council Complex on 12&nbsp;June&nbsp;2019.]]
A sit-in by the pro-democracy group [[Demosistō]] held at the [[Central Government Complex (Hong Kong)|Central Government Complex]] on 15&nbsp;March 2019 was the first protest against the extradition bill.<ref name="now_340955" /> The [[Civil Human Rights Front]] (CHRF), a platform for 50 [[pro-democracy camp (Hong Kong)|pro-democracy groups]], launched a protest march against the bill on 31&nbsp;March and another on 28&nbsp;April 2019.<ref name="hkfp_20190331_12000protest" /> The anti-extradition issue attracted more attention when pro-democratic lawmakers in the Legislative Council launched a [[filibuster]] campaign against the bill. In response, the Secretary of Security [[John Lee (government official)|John Lee]] announced that the government would resume second reading of the bill in full council on 12&nbsp;June 2019, bypassing the Bills Committee, whose role would have been to scrutinise it.<ref name="scmp_3011104" />

With the possibility of a second reading of the bill, the CHRF launched their third protest march on 9&nbsp;June. While police estimated attendance at the march on [[Hong Kong Island]] at 270,000, the organisers claimed that 1.03&nbsp;million people had attended the rally.<ref name="time_5603754" /><ref name="reuters_20191220_hkrevolt" /> Carrie Lam insisted second reading and debate over the bill be resumed on 12&nbsp;June.<ref name="govhk_20190609_response" /> Protesters [[612 Incident|successfully stopped the LegCo from resuming second reading of the bill]] by surrounding the LegCo Complex. Riot police dispersed protesters using controversial methods such as kettling, firing tear gas, bean bag rounds and rubber bullets, and allegedly assaulted journalists in the process.<ref name="scmp_3014104" /> Police Commissioner [[Stephen Lo]] declared the clashes a "riot".<ref name="govhk_20190612_policeaction" /> Police were subsequently criticised for using excessive force, such as firing tear gas at protesters at an approved rally,<ref name="independent_8957471" /><ref name="amnesty_20190621_policereport" /> and for the lack of identification numbers on police officers' uniforms.<ref name="amnesty_20190621_policeviolence" /> Following the clashes, protesters began calling for an independent inquiry into police brutality; they also urged the government to retract the "riot" characterisation.

[[File:190616 HK Protest Incendo 03.jpg|thumb|right|Organisers claimed two million attended the CHRF march on 16&nbsp;June&nbsp;2019, while the police put the figure at 338,000.]]
On 15&nbsp;June, Carrie Lam announced the bill's suspension but did not fully withdraw it.<ref name="scmp_3014643" /> A 35-year-old man committed suicide in protest of Lam's decision and police brutality on June 12.<ref name="hkfp_20190615_manjump" /> On the following day, CHRF claimed that 2&nbsp;million people had participated in the 16&nbsp;June protest, while the police estimated that there were 338,000 demonstrators at its peak.<ref name="scmp_3014695" /> Lam announced the suspension of the bill on 15&nbsp;June 2019,<ref name="scmp_3014669" />

===Storming of Legco and spillovers===
{{Main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (July 2019)}}
[[File:示威者於立法會會議廳展示標語, Jul 2019.jpg|left|thumb|Protesters briefly occupied the [[Legislative Council Complex]] on 1&nbsp;July&nbsp;2019.]]

The CHRF claimed a record turnout of 550,000 for their [[Hong Kong 1 July marches|annual march on 1&nbsp;July 2019]], while police estimated around 190,000 at the peak;<ref name="appledaily_59774211" /><ref name="hkfp_20190701_55000attend" /> an independent polling organisation estimated attendance at 260,000.<ref name="reuters_20190705_countingmarchers" /> The protest was largely peaceful. At night, partly angered by several more suicides since 15&nbsp;June 2019, some radical protesters [[Storming of the Legislative Council Complex|stormed into the Legislative Council]]; police took little action to stop them.<ref name="reuters_20190701_smashlegislature" /><ref name="cnn_20190701_newmanifesto" /><ref name="latimes_20190702_martyrnarrative" />

After 1&nbsp;July&nbsp;2019, protests spread to different areas in Hong Kong.<ref name="singtao_1038967" /><ref name="hkfp_20190705_moreprotests" /><ref name="hkfp_20190713_parralleltraders" /> The first anti-extradition protest in [[Kowloon]] was held on 7&nbsp;July.<ref name="nyt_20190707_chinesetourists" /> Once again, police officers' failure to display their [[warrant card]]s was a source of contention.<ref name="scmp_3017775" /> A peaceful protest on 14&nbsp;July in [[Sha Tin]] escalated into intense confrontations with the police when the protesters and other passers-by were kettled inside [[New Town Plaza]].<ref name="hkfp_20190715_policekettling" /><ref name="standardhk_131298" />

[[File:190727 HK Protest Incendo 02.jpg|thumb|Protesters marching on [[Castle Peak Road]] during the "Reclaim Yuen Long" protest on 27&nbsp;July&nbsp;2019 in response to the 21&nbsp;July Yuen Long Attack.]]
CHRF held another anti-extradition protest on 21&nbsp;July on Hong Kong Island. Instead of dispersing, protesters passed the police-mandated endpoint,<ref name="bbc_49061975" /> and headed for the [[Hong Kong Liaison Office|Liaison Office]] in [[Sai Ying Pun]], where they defaced the Chinese national emblem.<ref name="hkfp_20190722_condemndeface" /> While a standoff between the protesters and the police occurred on Hong Kong Island,<ref name="rthk_1469580" /> groups of white-clad individuals, suspected triad members, appeared and [[2019 Yuen Long attack|indiscriminately attacked people inside Yuen Long station]].<ref name="standardhk_131702" /> Police were absent during the attacks, and the local police stations were shuttered, leading to suspicion that the attack was coordinated with police. Pro-Beijing lawmaker [[Junius Ho]] was later seen greeting members of the group, which led to accusations that he approved of the attack.<ref name="standardhk_131702" /> The attack was often seen as the [[turning point]] for the movement, as it  crippled people's confidence in the police and turned a lot of citizens who were politically neutral or apathetic against the police.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Purbrick |first1=Martin |date=October 14, 2019 |title=A Report of the 2019 Hong Kong Protests|journal= [[Asian Affairs]]|volume= 50|issue=4 |pages= 465–487 |doi=10.1080/03068374.2019.1672397 |doi-access=free}}</ref>

On 27&nbsp;July, protesters marched in Yuen Long, despite opposition from rural groups and the police. The protest escalated into violent clashes inside Yuen Long station.<ref name="guardian_20190718_policethugs" /> The next day, protesters again defied the police ban and marched to [[Sai Wan]] and [[Causeway Bay]].<ref name="hkfp_20190729_49arrested" /> To support the arrestees charged with rioting, protesters rallied near the police stations in [[Kwai Chung]],<ref name="standardhk_210229" /> and [[Tin Shui Wai]], where protesters were attacked with fireworks launched from a moving vehicle.<ref name="hkfp_20190731_fireworkattack" /><ref name="coconutshk_20190731" />

===General strike and escalation===
{{Main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (August 2019)}}

Protesters returned to Mong Kok on 3&nbsp;August 2019, though some marched to block the [[Cross-Harbour Tunnel]] toll plaza in [[Hung Hom]].<ref name="hkfp_20190803_teargas" />  A call for a [[general strike]] on 5&nbsp;August was answered by about 350,000 people according to the [[Hong Kong Confederation of Trade Unions|Confederation of Trade Unions]];<ref name="rthk_1472997" /> over 200 flights had to be cancelled.<ref name="scmp_3021412" /><ref name="qz_1681247" /><ref name="hkfp_20190802_generalstrike" /> Protests were held in seven districts in Hong Kong. To disperse protesters, the police used more than 800 canisters of tear gas.<ref name="qz_1683024" /> Protesters in [[North Point]] were attacked by a group of stick-wielding men, leading to violent clashes.<ref name="scmp_3021571" /><ref name="standardhk_210378" />

[[File:20190807 Hong Kong anti-extradition bill protest (48480850197).jpg|thumb|left|Protesters pointing their laser pointers at a newspaper outside the [[Hong Kong Space Museum|Space Museum]], mocking an earlier police demonstration that aimed to illustrate the danger of laser pointers, which was seized from an arrested student-union president.<ref name="scmp_3021875" /><ref name="hkfp_20190807_shamshuipo" />]]

Various incidents involving alleged police brutality on 11&nbsp;August—police shot bean bag rounds that ruptured the eye of a female protester, the use of [[tear gas]] indoors, the deployment of undercover police as agents-provocateurs, and the firing of pepper ball rounds at protesters at a very close range—prompted protesters to stage a three-day sit-in at Hong Kong International Airport from 12 to 14&nbsp;August, forcing the [[Airport Authority Hong Kong|Airport Authority]] to cancel numerous flights.<ref name="rthk_1474188" /><ref name="hp_20190813_airporthostage" /><ref name="usatoday_1996574001" /> On 13&nbsp;August, protesters at the airport cornered, tied up and assaulted two men they accused of being either undercover police or agents for the mainland, who were later identified as a tourist and a ''[[Global Times]]'' reporter.<ref name="rthk_1474483" /><ref name="reuters_20190813_reporterheld" /> A peaceful rally was held in [[Victoria Park (Hong Kong)|Victoria Park]] by the CHRF on 18&nbsp;August to denounce police brutality. The CHRF claimed attendance of at least 1.7&nbsp;million people.<ref name="scmp_3022772" /> The police put peak attendance in the Victoria Park football areas at 128,000.

[[File:Hong Kong Way 20190823 17.jpg|thumb|On 23&nbsp;August, an estimated 210,000 people participated in the "[[Hong Kong Way]]" campaign to draw attention to the movement's [[#Objectives|five demands]]. The chain extended across the top of [[Lion Rock]].<ref name="qz_1693865" /><ref name="qz_1694046" />]]

Starting from the [[Kwun Tong]] protest on 24&nbsp;August, protesters began to target railway operator [[MTR]] after it closed four stations ahead of the legal, authorised protest.<ref name="hkfp_20190922_mtrprotestors" /> During the protests of 25&nbsp;August in [[Tsuen Wan]] and [[Kwai Tsing District]]s, hardline protesters threw bricks and [[Molotov cocktail|gasoline bombs]] toward the police who in turn responded with volleys of tear gas; police [[water cannon]] trucks were deployed for the first time.<ref name="wp_20190826_warningshot" /> During the protest, one officer fired an upward [[warning shot]], marking the first use of a live round since the demonstrations broke out in June.<ref name="wp_20190826_warningshot" /><ref name="bbc_20190830_gunshot" />

Ignoring a police ban, thousands of protesters took to the streets of Hong Kong Island on 31&nbsp;August following the arrests of high-profile pro-democracy activists and lawmakers the previous day.<ref name="scmp_3025020" /><ref name="nyt_20190831_protestsresume" /><ref name="wp_20190831_defiancearrests" /> At night, the Special Tactical Squad [[2019 Prince Edward station attack|stormed Prince Edward station]], where they beat and pepper-sprayed the commuters inside.<ref name="amnesty_20190901_policeprotest" /> Protesters rallied outside the Mong Kok police station in the following weeks to condemn police brutality and demanded the MTR Corporation release the CCTV footage of that night as rumours began to circulate on the internet that the police's operation had caused death, which they denied.<ref name="scmp_3025582" /><ref name="guardian_20190910_denyclaims" />

===Decision to withdraw the extradition bill===
{{main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (September 2019)}}
[[File:2019-09-08 Hong Kong anti-extradition bill protest 04.jpg|left|thumb|On 8&nbsp;September, the protesters marched to the US consulate to call for the passage of the [[Hong Kong Human Rights and Democracy Act]].<ref name="hkfp_20190915_urgelondon" />]]
On 4&nbsp;September, Carrie Lam announced the formal withdrawal of the extradition bill once Legco reconvened in October and the introduction of additional measures to calm the situation. However, protests continued to insist on all five demands being met.<ref name="hkfp_20190904_toolittle" /> During the month, protesters organised various flash mob rallies to sing the protest anthem "[[Glory to Hong Kong]]".<ref name="reuters_20190910_anthembooed" /><ref name="reuters_20190909_humanchain" /> They continued their attempts to disrupt the airport's operations,<ref name="hkfp_20190907_interceptprotest" /> and held pop-up mall protests, which targeted shops and corporations perceived to be pro-Beijing.<ref name="cnn_20190922_chaosspread" />

[[File:2019-09-29 全球反極權大遊行 Anti-totalitarianism rally (Hong Kong) 028.jpg|thumb|right|The police using [[water cannon]] trucks outside the Government HQ during the 29&nbsp;September&nbsp;2019 march.]]
A mass protest on 15&nbsp;September descended into chaos in North Point as a group of locals, which allegedly included a sizeable number of [[Fujian gang|people of Fujianese origin]], physically assaulted protesters.<ref name="hkfp_20190916_bloodynight" /> Carrie Lam held the first public meeting in [[Queen Elizabeth Stadium]] in Wan Chai with 150 members of the public. Protesters demanding to talk to her surrounded the venue and trapped her inside for four hours.<ref name="reuters_20190926_opendialogue" /> On 29 September, there was an anti-[[Chinese Communist Party]] rally in defiance of a police ban. Solidarity protests were held on the same day in over 40 cities around the world.<ref name="hkfp_20190930_antitotalitarianism" />

===National Day and invocation of emergency law===
{{main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (October 2019)}}
[[File:2019-10-01 Demonstration Hong Kong 11.jpg|thumb|left|Hong Kong protesters threw eggs at the portrait of [[General Secretary of the Communist Party of China]] and [[paramount leader]] of China [[Xi Jinping]] on 1&nbsp;October&nbsp;2019 during the 70th [[National Day of the People's Republic of China]].]]
On 1&nbsp;October 2019, mass protests and violent conflict occurred between the protesters and police in various districts of Hong Kong during the [[70th anniversary of the People's Republic of China|70th&nbsp;anniversary of the founding of the People's Republic of China]]. This resulted in the first use of live rounds by police. One 18-year-old student protester was shot in the chest by police in [[Tsuen Wan]] while trying to hit a policeman with a rod.<ref name="abc_11565024" /><ref name="scmp_3031325" /><ref name="aljazeera_191002005617929" /> The police fired around 1,400 tear gas canisters and made 269 arrests on one day, setting a record for both since the protests began in June.<ref name="hkfp_20191002_singledayrecord" />

[[File:P1111040 (48870971291).jpg|thumb|right|Protesters setting up a makeshift roadblock ignited with fire in [[Causeway Bay]] on 6&nbsp;October 2019 in a march protesting against the invocation of the emergency law.]]
Carrie Lam invoked the [[Emergency Regulations Ordinance]] to impose a [[anti-mask law|law to ban wearing face masks in public gatherings]], attempting to curb the ongoing protests on 4&nbsp;October.<ref name="bbc_49931598" /> The law's enactment was followed by continued demonstrations in various districts of Hong Kong, blocking major thoroughfares, vandalising shops perceived to be pro-Beijing and paralysing the MTR system.<ref name="vox_20898568" /><ref name="reuters_20191004_banemergency" /><ref name="hkfp_20191004_14yoshot" /> Protests and citywide flash mob rallies persisted throughout the month.<ref name="standardhk_135533" /><ref name="scmp_3035804" />

Secretary for Security John Lee officially withdrew the extradition bill on 23&nbsp;October,<ref name="hkfp_20191023_officiallywithdrawn" /> but this has failed to stop the protesters who believed that the government was inadequate in responding to their demands.

===Intensification and sieges of the universities===
{{main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (November 2019)}}

[[Doxing]] uncovered the details of a police officer's wedding in Tseung Kwan O which were leaked.<ref name="scmp_3037001" /> Protesters intending to crash the event set up roadblocks around [[Sheung Tak Estate]] and clashed with the police late at night on 3&nbsp;November 2019. Alex Chow Tsz-lok, a 22-year-old student at the [[Hong Kong University of Science and Technology]] (HKUST), was later found unconscious on the second floor of the estate's car park. He died on 8&nbsp;November following two unsuccessful brain surgeries.<ref name="mingpao_1572893617823" /><ref name="scmp_3036833" /> After his death, protesters engaged in flash mob rallies against the police and attended vigils in various districts of Hong Kong. They blamed the police for his death, though the police denied any involvement.<ref name="nyt_20191107_studentdies" />

[[File:CUHK Protester face with smoke 20191112.jpg|thumb|left|The police confronted the protesters at the entrance of the [[Chinese University of Hong Kong]] on 12&nbsp;November&nbsp;2019.]]

In response to Chow's death, protesters planned a city-wide strike starting on 11&nbsp;November. They disrupted transport in the morning in various districts of Hong Kong.<ref name="hkfp_20191111_liverounds" /> That morning, a policeman fired live rounds in [[Sai Wan Ho]], wounding an unarmed 21-year-old.<ref name="reuters_20191111_openfire" /> In the afternoon, police also fired tear gas in [[Central, Hong Kong|Central]], Hong Kong's [[Central Business District]], during a lunchtime protest, causing businesses to close early.<ref name="yahoo_123212403" /> On 14&nbsp;November, an elderly man died from a head injury which he had sustained the previous day during a confrontation between protesters and government supporters in [[Sheung Shui]].<ref name="cnn_20191114_elderlydies" /><ref name="today-emh" />

For the first time, during a standoff on 11&nbsp;November, police shot numerous rounds of tear gas, sponge grenades and rubber bullets into the campuses of universities, while protesters threw bricks and petrol bombs in response.<ref name="scmp_3037262" /> Student protesters from the [[Chinese University of Hong Kong]] (CUHK) [[2019 Chinese University of Hong Kong conflict|confronted the police]] for two consecutive days.<ref name="hkfp_20191112_cuhkbattleground" /> After the conflict, protesters briefly occupied several universities, which became their strongholds as they crafted various improvised offensive weapons inside.<ref name="scmp_3037990" /><ref name="npr_779399836" /> A major conflict between protesters and police took place in Hung Hom on 17&nbsp;November after protesters took control of the [[Hong Kong Polytechnic University]] (PolyU) and blockaded the Cross-Harbour Tunnel. Thus began the [[siege of the Hong Kong Polytechnic University|siege of PolyU]] by police which ended with them storming onto the campus and arresting several protesters and volunteer medics in the early morning of 18&nbsp;November.<ref name="rthk_1492855" /><ref name="smh_p53bgw" /> Various escape attempts were thwarted by police.<ref name="bbc_50455727" />

[[File:DSCF5290 (49094872852).jpg|thumb|right|Protesters in [[Yau Ma Tei]] on 18&nbsp;November&nbsp;2019 as they attempted to breach the police's cordon line to break through to protesters trapped inside [[Hong Kong Polytechnic University]].]]
With PolyU under complete lockdown by police, and students inside running short of supplies, protesters outside the campus attempted to penetrate police cordons to break through to those trapped inside but were repelled by tear gas and [[Pepper-spray projectile|pepper balls]].<ref name="asiatimes_20191118_siegecrisis" /> Police action in [[Yau Ma Tei]] resulted in a stampede which was denied by police but confirmed by firefighters.<ref name="rthk_1493235" /> Hygiene on campus quickly deteriorated, and the protesters inside reported being mentally and physically weak.<ref name="scmp_3039102" /> More than 1,100 people were arrested in and around PolyU over the course of the siege.<ref name="scmp_3039868" /><ref name="hkfp_20191129_liftsiege" /> The siege was ended on 29&nbsp;November.<ref name="scmp_3039681" />

=== Electoral landslide and New Year's Day protests ===
{{Main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (December 2019)|l1=Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (December 2019|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (January 2020)|l2=January 2020)}}
[[File:DSCF8170 (49288827278).jpg|thumb|left|Protesters gathered in a shopping mall in [[Sheung Shui]] on 28&nbsp;December&nbsp;2019 to protest the influence of [[Parallel trading in Hong Kong|parallel trading]] and cross-border shopping.]]
The [[2019 Hong Kong local elections|24&nbsp;November 2019 District Council election]], considered a referendum on the government and the protests, attracted a record high [[voter turnout]].<ref name="reuters_20191215_councilelections" /> The results saw the pro-democracy camp win by a landslide, with the pro-Beijing camp suffering their greatest electoral defeat in Hong Kong's history.<ref name="guardian_20191124_landslidevictory" /><ref name="nyt_20191124_backerswin" /> The unprecedented electoral success of the pro-democracy voters, the mass arrests during the PolyU siege, and faster response by police contributed to a decrease in the intensity and frequency of the protests in December 2019 and January 2020.<ref name="20200123hongkongfpA" />

Protesters returned to the streets on 1&nbsp;December in Tsim Sha Tsui to reiterate their five demands, but the police fired volleys of tear gas into the crowd and revoked the Letter of No Objection one hour after the march began,<ref name="scmp_3040135" /> after alleging that protesters were throwing [[smoke bombs]].<ref name="turnout" /> In an 8&nbsp;December mass march held to maintain pressure on the government, more than 800,000 protesters came to the streets, according to the organiser CHRF. Meanwhile, police reported the peak turnout at 183,000. The CHRF-organised march was its first permitted by police in nearly four months.<ref name="cnn_20191208_protesterspressure" />
[[File:DSCF8925 (49311981367).jpg|thumb|right|Protesters flood the streets during the New Year's march.]]
The rest of December saw the return of pop-up mall protests, with clashes occurring in several major shopping centres around the city. These pop-up protests continued during the Christmas season.<ref name="reuters_20191224_xmasprotest" /><ref name="facebook_10886856774782522" /> On 1&nbsp;January 2020, a protest was held by the CHRF in support of the protesters' demands.<ref name="rthk_1500493" /> Organisers claimed over 1,030,000 people participated in the protest, while police said peak attendance was 60,000.<ref name="rthk_1500561" /> Police cut the march short and responded with tear gas after protesters vandalised [[HSBC Building (Hong Kong)|HSBC's headquarters]] and several HSBC branches in retaliation for the [[HSBC|bank]] and the police seizing over HK$70&nbsp;million (US$9&nbsp;million) in donations for the protests from [[Spark Alliance]] in December.<ref name="scmp_3043325" /><ref name="scmp3046718" /><ref>{{cite web|url=https://time.com/5757324/hong-kong-new-years-day-protests/|title=As Hong Kong's Leader Pledges to 'Listen Humbly,' Protesters Return to the Streets|work=[[Time (magazine)|Time]]|date=1 January 2020|access-date=29 November 2020}}</ref> An HSBC branch had already been vandalized by protesters on 24 December for the same reason.<ref name="rthk_1499509"/>

===COVID-19 crisis===
{{Main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (February 2020)|l1=Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (February|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (March 2020)|l2=March|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (April 2020)|l3=April 2020)}}
{{Further|COVID-19 pandemic in Hong Kong}}
[[File:Fai Ming Estate lobby fire view2 20200126.png|thumb|left|A fire was started inside the lobby of [[Fai Ming Estate]], [[Fanling]] after the government proposed to use the [[public housing estate]] as a [[quarantine]] facility.]]

The outbreak of the [[COVID-19 pandemic]] in mainland China caused the number of large-scale rallies to dwindle further because of fears that they might facilitate the spread of the virus. Despite this, the pro-democratic movement's tactics were repurposed to pressure the government to take stronger actions to safeguard Hong Kong's [[public health]] in the face of the [[2020 coronavirus outbreak in Hong Kong|coronavirus outbreak in Hong Kong]].<ref name="vox21124157" /> Protesters demanded all mainland travellers be banned from entering Hong Kong. From 3&nbsp;to 7&nbsp;February 2020, hospital staff launched a labour strike with the same goal.<ref name="bbc51349154" /><ref name="scmp3049512" /> The strike was partially successful as Lam, despite rejecting a full border closure, only left three of the 14 crossing points with mainland China open.<ref name="wp_20200204" />

People responded negatively to the government's attempt to set up quarantine and clinical centres in neighbourhoods close to residents and marched to express their discontent or blocked roads to thwart the government's plans across the territory.<ref name="theatlantic605662" /><ref name="wsj_11580475635" /> Between late January and early February, [[improvised explosive devices]] were found in several locations, and petrol bombs were thrown at four police stations and a patrol car, in a wave of action over the government's failure to close the city's border and supply protective gear.<ref name="scmp3049080" />

As the coronavirus crisis escalated in February and March 2020, the scale of the protests dwindled further.<ref name="20200315theguardian" /><ref name="20200225hongkongfp" /> Police have used coronavirus laws banning groups of more than four, for example, to disperse protesters.<ref name="UAWgq" /><ref name="EMbMa" /> On 18 April, police arrested 15 pro-democracy activists including [[Jimmy Lai]], [[Martin Lee]] and [[Margaret Ng]] for their activities in 2019, drawing international condemnation.<ref name="F0e4K" /> On 15 May, a 21-year-old young man surnamed Sin was sentenced to four years in prison for his participation in the [[612 Incident|12 June protest]], becoming the first person to be jailed for the charge of [[rioting]] since the protest movement started.<ref name="lTsEE" />

===Implementation of the national security law===
{{main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (May 2020)|l1=Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (May|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (June 2020)|l2=June|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (July 2020)|l3=July 2020)}}
[[File:Tear Gas in CWB Hennessy Road View 20200524.png|thumb|left|Tear gas was used for the first time in two months on 24 May as thousands of protesters marched against both the [[National Anthem Bill]] and the national security law]]
<!--
In October 2019, the pro-democratic bloc in the LegCo launched a filibuster campaign led by vice-chairman [[Dennis Kwok]], who presided over the LegCo meetings, to stall the House Committee election procedures and thereby the second reading of the [[National Anthem Bill]], a bill that was considered to be "evil" by the democrats.<ref name="scmp3081494" /><ref name="qJuBc" /> A [[Hong Kong Watch]] article opined that the filibustering was the only strategy available to the democrats, because of their marginalisation in the LegCo despite them representing the majority of the population.<ref name="20200508hongkongwatch" /> The directors of the Chinese [[Hong Kong and Macau Affairs Office]] (HKMAO) and the [[Hong Kong Liaison Office|Liaison Office]] threatened to disqualify Kwok. Their actions were controversial as Article 22 of the [[Hong Kong Basic Law]] states that no department of the [[Central People's Government]] can interfere in Hong Kong's affairs. The two directors claimed that the two departments were exempted, and this was agreed by Lam's administration despite saying otherwise in the past.<ref name="scmp3081816" /> Pro-Beijing lawmaker [[Starry Lee]] was re-elected House Committee chairwoman on 18 May after security guards removed most pro-democracy members due to a violent scuffle before the vote was called.<ref name="Fzgg5" />
-->
On 21 May 2020, state media announced that the [[Standing Committee of the National People's Congress]] (NPCSC) would begin [[National People's Congress Decision on Hong Kong national security legislation|drafting]] [[Hong Kong national security law|a new law]] that covers "secession, foreign interference, terrorism and subversion against the central government", to be added into the Annex III of the Hong Kong Basic Law. This meant that the law would come into effect through [[promulgation]], bypassing local legislation.<ref name="20200521hongkongfp" /> Observers considered this as China's boldest step yet to bring Hong Kong under its control, and accused it of depreciating the "one country, two systems" principle in violation of the terms of the [[Sino-British Joint Declaration]]. They expected that it would have far-reaching effects on the city's freedom of expression and future economic success.<ref name="20200521nytimes" /><ref name="aljazeera2005220322" /> The UK, along with Australia, Canada, and the US also issued a joint statement expressing their deep concern regarding the National Security Law on 28 May.<ref name="ADQPF" /> Despite international pressure, the NPCSC passed the national security legislation on 28 May.<ref name="bbc52829176" /><ref name="aJvjP" />

[[File:Hong Kong IMG 20190812 170957 (48520377932).jpg|thumb|The national security law banned anyone from shouting or displaying the slogan "[[Liberate Hong Kong, the revolution of our times]]" ({{Lang-zh|link=no|t=光復香港，時代革命}}), which has achieved mainstream adoption since July 2019.<ref name="slogan explainer" /><ref name="PZmlS" />]]
The draft sparked increased protests: the mass march on 24 May in Causeway Bay was the largest protest since the beginning of the pandemic, as civilians responded to online calls to march against both the National Anthem Bill and the proposed national security law.<ref name="20200524cnbc" /> For the first time in two months, the police deployed tear gas in an attempt to disperse the protesters.<ref name="20200524bloomberg" /> On 27 May at least 396 people were arrested during a day-long protest across Hong Kong over the national anthem bill's reading on the same day and the anti-sedition law; most of the arrested were taken into custody even before any protest actions had begun.<ref name="scmp3087451" />

On 30 June, the NPCSC passed the national security law unanimously, without informing the public and the local officials of the content of the law.<ref name="cPPxk" /> The law creates a [[chilling effect]] in the city. [[Demosistō]], which had been involved in lobbying for other nations' support, and several [[Hong Kong independence|pro-independent]] groups announced that they had decided to disband and cease all operations, fearing that they would be the targets of the new law.<ref name="aXz4I" /> Thousands of protesters showed up on July 1 to protest against the newly implemented law. On that day, the police arrested at least ten people for breaching national security as they deemed that individuals who displayed or possessed flags, placards and phone stickers with protest slogans or other [[protest art]] have already committed the crime of "subverting the country".<ref name="B75MR" />

Following the implementation of the national security law, the international community reassessed their policies towards China.

* On 3 July 2020, Canada announced it was suspending its extradition treaty with Hong Kong over the introduction of the national security law.<ref>{{cite news |last= The Canadian Press |first= The Canadian Press |date=3 July 2020 |title=Canada suspends extradition treaty with Hong Kong over new security law|url= https://www.cbc.ca/news/politics/canada-suspending-extradition-treaty-hong-kong-over-security-law-1.5636479|work=[[Canadian Broadcasting Corporation|CBC]] |location= |access-date=28 July 2020}}</ref> This was followed shortly after by Australia,<ref>{{cite news |last= Needham |first= Kirsty |date=9 July 2020 |title= Angering China, Australia suspends extradition treaty with Hong Kong, extends visas|url= https://www.theguardian.pe.ca/news/world/australian-pm-suspends-extradition-treaty-extends-visas-for-hong-kong-citizens-471269/|work=[[The Guardian]] |location= |access-date=28 July 2020}}</ref> the United Kingdom,<ref>{{cite news |last= AFP |first= AFP |date=21 July 2020 |title= UK suspends extradition treaty with Hong Kong|url= https://guardian.ng/news/world/uk-suspends-extradition-treaty-with-hong-kong/|work=[[The Guardian]] |location= |access-date=28 July 2020}}</ref> New Zealand,<ref>{{cite news |last= Graham-McLay |first= Charlotte |date=28 July 2020 |title=New Zealand suspends Hong Kong extradition treaty over China national security law|url= https://www.theguardian.com/world/2020/jul/28/new-zealand-suspends-hong-kong-extradition-treaty-over-china-national-security-law|work=[[The Guardian]] |location= |access-date=28 July 2020}}</ref> and Germany.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/07/31/just-in-germany-suspends-extradition-treaty-with-hong-kong-minister/|title=Germany suspends extradition treaty with Hong Kong citing election delay – minister|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=July 31, 2020|access-date=August 1, 2020}}</ref>
* The US Congress passed the [[Hong Kong Autonomy Act]] and president [[Donald Trump]] had signed an Executive Order to revoke the city's special trade status, after  [[United States Secretary of State|Secretary of State]] [[Mike Pompeo]] informed the Congress that Hong Kong was no longer autonomous from China and so should be considered the same country in trade and other such matters.<ref name="bbc52824839" /> 
*Australia promised to provide "safe haven" visas for Hongkongers who were at risk of political prosecution and offered a pathway to permanent residency.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/asia/australasia/article/3092827/we-cannot-guarantee-residency-hongkongers-australian-minister|title=We cannot guarantee residency for Hongkongers, Australian minister says, days after 'safe haven' offer|work=[[South China Morning Post]] / [[Deutsche Presse-Agentur|dpa]]|date=12 July 2020|access-date=29 July 2020}}</ref> 
*[[British Home Office]] announced that starting from early 2021, current and former holders of the BN(O) passport in Hong Kong can apply for the "Hong Kong BN(O) visa" online, allowing them to resettle in the UK along with their dependents for five years before they become eligible to apply for permanent citizenship.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/china/diplomacy/article/3094298/britain-unveils-details-citizenship-offer-hongkongers-bno|title=Britain unveils details of citizenship offer for Hongkongers with BN(O) passports|first=Stuart|last=Lau|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|date=July 22, 2020|access-date=July 29, 2020}}</ref> 
*The [[European Union]] also announced plans to restrict the export of technology that can be used for "internal repression, interception of internal communications or cyber surveillance" to Hong Kong.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.dw.com/en/eu-restricts-exports-to-hong-kong-over-china-security-law/a-54359734|title=EU restricts exports to Hong Kong over China security law|work=[[Deutsche Welle]]|date=July 28, 2020|access-date=July 29, 2020}}</ref>
* On [[Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (August 2020)|7 August]], the US announced that they would impose sanctions on eleven Hong Kong and Chinese top officials, including Chief Executive [[Carrie Lam]], for undermining Hong Kong's freedom and autonomy.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2020/aug/07/carrie-lam-us-sanctions-trump-hong-kong-security-law|title=US imposes sanctions on leader Carrie Lam over Hong Kong crackdown|first=Emma|last=Graham-Harrison|work=[[CNN]]|date=August 7, 2020|access-date=August 12, 2020}}</ref> In response, China imposed sanctions on six US lawmakers and five leaders from [[human rights organisations]] and advocacy groups.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://edition.cnn.com/2020/08/10/politics/china-sanctions-rubio-cruz-cotton-smith/index.html|title=China sanctions Rubio, Cruz and other US officials for 'Hong Kong-related issues'|first=Chandelis|last=Duster|work=[[CNN]]|date=August 10, 2020|access-date=August 12, 2020}}</ref>

===Subsequent suppression===
{{main|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (August 2020)|l1=Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (August|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (September 2020)|l2=September|Timeline of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests (October 2020)|l3=October 2020)}}
[[File:Civic Party vote for 2020 legislative election 20200619.png|thumb|left|[[Civic Party]] promoting the primaries as the democrats aspired to secure a majority in LegCo. Four candidates from the party were disqualified by the government.]]

Invigorated by its success in the November 2019 District Council election, the pro-democratic bloc was eyeing to win over half of the 70 seats in the Legislative Council in the [[2020 Hong Kong legislative election|election]] set to be held on 6 September.<ref name="vTtQu" /> To achieve this, the pro-democratic camp held [[2020 Hong Kong pro-democracy primaries|primaries]] for the first time.<ref name="tohold" /> Some members from the pro-democratic camp, in particular, the more radical democrats, promised to use all constitutional powers of LegCo members to force the government to concede and respond to all of the five demands.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://asiatimes.com/2020/07/hk-activists-budget-plan-is-subversion-beijing/|title=HK activists' budget plan is subversion: Beijing|first=Frank|last=Chen|work=[[Asia Times]]|date=July 15, 2020|access-date=July 31, 2020}}</ref> Unfazed by the national security law, more than 600,000 people cast their votes in mid July 2020. In the primaries, traditional parties lost ground in most of the direct constituencies to the localists and radical democrats. Beijing's offices in Hong Kong declared the primaries to be "illegal" and later to be a "blatant challenge" to the Basic Law and national security law, forcing the organisers to withdraw from further election coordination.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/07/14/serious-provocation-beijing-blasts-hong-kong-democrat-primaries-after-initial-results-reveal/|title='Serious provocation': Beijing blasts Hong Kong democrat primaries after initial results revealed|first=Kelly|last=Ho|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=July 14, 2020|access-date=July 29, 2020}}</ref><ref name="GV96h" />

The Hong Kong government [[2020 Hong Kong LegCo candidates' disqualification controversy|disqualified twelve candidates]] on 30 July, nearly all of them were winners from the pro-democratic primaries. In an unexpected move, four incumbent lawmakers who were generally considered to be more moderate, were also disqualified from running in the election by the returning officers.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/07/30/breaking-hong-kong-bans-8-pro-democracy-figures-from-legislative-election/|title=Hong Kong bans Joshua Wong and 11 other pro-democracy figures from legislative election|first=Kelly|last=Ho|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=July 30, 2020|access-date=July 30, 2020}}</ref><ref name="GV96h" /> The decision drew international condemnation for obstructing the election and the democratic process.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/article/3095413/hong-kong-elections-candidate-disqualification-faces-international|title=Hong Kong elections: candidate disqualification faces international criticism|first=Stuart|last=Lau|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|date=July 31, 2020|access-date=August 1, 2020}}</ref> On the following day, Carrie Lam, going against the public opinion,<ref>{{cite web|url= https://www.thestandnews.com/politics/民研-55-市民支持立法會選舉如期舉行-黎恩灝-押後續損民主價值/ |title=民研：55% 市民支持立法會選舉如期舉行 黎恩灝：押後續損民主價值|work=[[Stand News]]|date=July 31, 2020|access-date=August 1, 2020}}</ref> invoked emergency powers to [[Postponement of the 2020 Hong Kong legislative election|delay the election]] to September 2021, citing the [[COVID-19 pandemic in Hong Kong|pandemic]] as the reason. The pan-democratic bloc critisied the decision, believing that it is politically motivated. The NPCSC passed a motion to extend the incumbent 6th Legislative Council (which has a pro-Beijing majority) for no less than one year. Some activists considered the NPCSC's decision as unlawful, resulting in a [[Postponement of the 2020 Hong Kong legislative election#Stay-or-go debate|stay-or-go debate]] that ultimately ended with the departure of three pro-democratic lawmakers.<ref>{{cite news|title=Most of Hong Kong's opposition lawmakers to serve out extended term in Legislative Council, after supporters narrowly back move in poll|first1=Natalie|last1=Wong|first2=Tony|last2=Cheung|date=29 September 2020|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/politics/article/3103434/hong-kong-opposition-poll-shows-supporters-narrowly-back}}</ref> While the NPCSC allowed the four disqualified incumbent lawmakers to transition to the extended term in July, they decided to remove them from office in November 2020, resulting in the mass resignation of all of opposition lawmakers.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/politics/article/3109454/mass-resignation-hong-kong-opposition-lawmakers-after|title=Mass resignation of Hong Kong opposition lawmakers after Beijing rules on disqualification|first=Tony|last=Leung|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|date=November 11, 2020|accessdate=December 2, 2020}}</ref>

[[File:Secretary Pompeo Meets with Nathan Law (50138297826).jpg|thumb|right|Former lawmaker [[Nathan Law]] met with US Secretary of State [[Mike Pompeo]] in July 2020, discussing matters concerning the national security law, the pro-democratic primaries, and the LegCo election.]]

[[Tony Chung]], a pro-independence activist, and several students aged 16 to 21 were arrested on 29 July for "inciting secession", becoming the first group of citizens to be arrested under the new law outside of arrests made during protests.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/law-and-crime/article/3095240/least-three-core-members-hong-kong-pro-independence|title=Four members of Hong Kong pro-independence group arrested by police officers from national security unit|first=Danny|last=Mok|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|date=July 29, 2020|access-date=July 30, 2020}}</ref> On 30 July, state media announced that the Hong Kong police had issued [[arrest warrant]]s to six exiled activists for breaching the national security law. These individuals included former lawmaker [[Nathan Law]], former British consulate employee [[Simon Cheng]], pro-independence activist [[Ray Wong]], and United States activist [[Samuel Chu]], with the arrest warrant of Chu being the first case of [[extraterritorial jurisdiction]] that is claimed by the law.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/07/31/hong-kong-police-order-arrest-of-nathan-law-and-4-other-exiled-activists-state-media/|title=Hong Kong police order arrest of Nathan Law and other exiled activists – state media|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=July 31, 2020|access-date=August 1, 2020}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2020/jul/31/china-hong-kong-security-law-american-citizen-exiles?CMP=share_btn_tw|title=China uses Hong Kong security law against US and UK-based activists|first=Adam|last=Gabbatt|work=[[The Guardian]]|date=August 1, 2020|access-date=August 1, 2020}}</ref> The police continued to use the law to target local activists and critics of Beijing. On 10 August, business tycoon [[Jimmy Lai]] and activist [[Agnes Chow]] were arrested. The police also [[police raid|raided]] the headquarters of ''[[Apple Daily]]'', which is owned by Lai's [[Next Digital]].<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2020/08/hong-kong-media-tycoon-lai-released-bail-arrest-200811173810887.html|title=Hong Kong media tycoon Lai, activist Agnes Chow released on bail|work=[[Al Jazeera]]|date=August 11, 2020|access-date=August 12, 2020}}</ref> [[2020 detainment of Hong Kong residents at sea by China|Twelve Hong Kong activists]] who were released on bail were captured by China's Coast Guard Bureau while fleeing to [[Taiwan]] on a speedboat on August 23. Detained in [[Yantian]], [[Shenzhen]], they were subsequently charged with crossing the Chinese border illegally, and were prevented from choosing their lawyers and meeting their families.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/politics/article/3101582/hong-kong-protests-carrie-lam-says-calling-12-detained|title=Hong Kong protests: Carrie Lam says calling 12 detained in Shenzhen 'democracy activists' a bid to distract from wanted status|first=Jack|last=Lau|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|date=September 15, 2020|access-date=October 7, 2020}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://www1.cbn.com/cbnnews/world/2020/september/will-we-see-them-alive-again-families-of-12-detained-hong-kong-youths-fear-for-their-lives|title=Will We See Them Alive Again? Families of 12 Detained Hong Kong Youths Fear for Their Lives|first=Lucille|last=Talusan|work=[[CBN News]]|date=September 18, 2020|access-date=October 7, 2020}}</ref> Meanwhile, democracy campaigner [[Joshua Wong]] and fellow activists [[Agnes Chow]] and [[Ivan Lam]] were  jailed by a court in West Kowloon and will be sentenced within a week of the guilty pleas.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2020/11/23/world/asia/joshua-wong-agnes-chow-hong-kong.html|title=Joshua Wong Pleads Guilty Over 2019 Hong Kong Protest|accessdate= 23 November 2020|website=The New York Times}}</ref>
[[Joshua Wong]] is sentenced to 13 months of imprisonment over an unauthorised protest  in front of police headquarters in June 2019. Activist [[Agnes Chow]] and [[Ivan Lam]] are sentenced to ten months and seven months respectively.<ref>{{cite_web|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2020/dec/02/hong-kong-activist-joshua-wong-jailed-over-protest-police-hq|title=Hong Kong activist Joshua Wong jailed for 13 and a half months over protest|accessdate=2 December 2020|website=The Guardian}}</ref>

As protest activities dwindled, the government continued to tighten its control in Hong Kong, from censoring school textbooks and removing any mention of the [[Tiananmen massacre]],<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/08/19/hong-kong-teachers-union-raises-concerns-over-censorship-as-publishers-revise-textbooks-after-govt-review/|title=Hong Kong teachers' union raises concerns over censorship as publishers revise textbooks after gov't review|first=Kelly|last=Ho|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=August 19, 2020|access-date=September 4, 2020}}</ref> to removing public examination questions which the authorities deemed politically inappropriate,<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/12/01/hong-kong-official-who-resigned-over-history-exam-question-reveals-immense-political-pressure/|title=Hong Kong official who resigned over history exam question reveals ‘immense political pressure’|first=Selina|last=Cheng|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=December 1, 2020|accessdate=December 2, 2020}}</ref> to deregistering "yellow-ribbon" teachers,<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/10/06/hong-kong-teacher-struck-off-for-allegedly-promoting-independence-as-lam-vows-more-action-against-bad-apples/|title=Hong Kong teacher struck off for allegedly promoting independence as Lam vows more action against 'bad apples'|first=Rachel|last=Wong|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=October 6, 2020|access-date=October 7, 2020}}</ref> to declaring that [[separation of powers]] never existed in Hong Kong despite previous comments by the city's top judges recognising its importance in Hong Kong.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/09/01/no-separation-of-powers-in-hong-kong-says-chief-exec-carrie-lam-despite-previous-comments-from-top-judges/|title=No separation of powers in Hong Kong says Chief Exec. Carrie Lam, despite previous comments from top judges|first=Jennifer|last=Creery|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=September 1, 2020|access-date=September 4, 2020}}</ref> It also attempted to reshape the narrative of the Yuen Long attack by claiming that the attack had not been indiscriminate, changing the officially reported police response time, and arresting [[Lam Cheuk-ting]], a pro-democracy lawmaker who was hurt in the attack, for "rioting".<ref>{{cite news |title=Two Hong Kong democrats arrested over 2019 protests; Lam Cheuk-ting detained over alleged 'rioting' during Yuen Long mob attack |url=https://hongkongfp.com/2020/08/26/two-hong-kong-democrats-arrested-over-2019-protests-lam-cheuk-ting-detained-over-alleged-rioting-during-yuen-long-mob-attack/ |access-date=3 September 2020 |work=26 August 2020}}</ref> In August 2020, the [[Department of Justice (Hong Kong)|Department of Justice]], under the leadership of [[Teresa Cheng (politician)|Teresa Cheng]], intervened and dismissed two private prosecutions launched by pro-democracy lawmaker [[Ted Hui Chi-fung]] against a driver who rammed into a protester in October 2019 and a police officer who shot and seriously injured a protester in November 2019.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/law-and-crime/article/3098628/hong-kong-justice-department-wins-court-approval-quash|title=Hong Kong court throws out two private prosecutions tied to social unrest|first=Brian|last=Wong|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|date=August 24, 2020|access-date=September 13, 2020}}</ref>

==Clashes between protesters and counter-protesters==
{{multiple image|caption_align=center|header_align=center
 | align = right
 | total_width = 300

| image1 = 香港民陣再發動4-28反引渡條例大遊行 03 (cropped).jpg
 | caption1 = [[Jimmy Sham]], a protest organiser and the convenor of [[Civil Human Rights Front|CHRF]], was attacked twice during the protests.

| image2 = Junius Ho Kwan-yiu.jpg
 | caption2 = Pro-Beijing lawmaker [[Junius Ho]], who defended the assailants of the [[2019 Yuen Long attack|Yuen Long attack]],<ref name="QcPLw" /> was stabbed.
}}

Clashes between protesters and counter-protesters had become more frequent since the movement began in June 2019. During a pro-police rally on 30&nbsp;June, their supporters began directing profanities at their opposition counterparts and destroyed their [[Lennon Wall]] and the memorial for Marco Leung, leading to intense confrontations between the two camps.<ref name="hkfp_20190630_lennonwall" /> Pro-Beijing citizens, wearing "I love HK police" T-shirts and waving the Chinese national flag, assaulted people perceived to be protesters on 14&nbsp;September in [[Fortress Hill]].<ref name="aljazeera_190914140138713" /> Lennon Walls became sites of conflict between the two camps, with pro-Beijing citizens attempting to tear down the messages or removing [[poster art]].<ref name="scmp_3018174" /><ref name="bbc_48946993" /> Some protesters and pedestrians were beaten and attacked with [[knife attack|knives]] near Lennon Walls by a single perpetrator<ref name="scmp_3020340" /><ref name="hkfp_20190820_criticalcondition" /> or by suspected gang members.<ref name="scmp3048395" /> A reporter was stabbed and a teenager distributing pro-protest leaflets had his abdomen slashed.<ref name="rthk_1487133" /> Owners of small businesses seen to be supportive of the protests and their employees have been assaulted in suspected politically motivated attacks and their businesses vandalized.<ref>{{cite news |title=Owners of Yuen Long 'yellow restaurant' attacked in possibly politically motivated assault |url=https://coconuts.co/hongkong/news/owners-of-yuen-long-yellow-restaurant-attacked-in-possibly-politically-motivated-assault/ |access-date=3 September 2020 |date=29 June 2020}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |title=Chef at pro-protest restaurant Lung Mun Cafe attacked in bloody assault |url=https://coconuts.co/hongkong/news/chef-at-pro-protest-restaurant-lung-mun-cafe-attacked-in-bloody-assault/ |access-date=3 September 2020 |date=6 July 2020}}</ref>

Some civilians allegedly attempted to ram their cars into crowds of protesters or the barricades they set up.<ref name="coconutshk_20190805" /><ref name="hkfp_20191006_outsideoffices" /> In one instance, a female protester suffered severe thigh fractures.<ref name="20191010hongkongfp" /> Protest organisers, including [[Jimmy Sham]] from the CHRF, and pro-democratic lawmakers such as [[Roy Kwong]] were assaulted and attacked.<ref name="hkfp_20190924_royattacked" /><ref name="hkfp_20190829_maxbeaten" /><ref name="hkfp_20191016_jimmyattacked" /> On 3&nbsp;November, politician [[Andrew Chiu]] had his ear bitten off by a Chinese mainlander who had reportedly knifed three other people outside [[Cityplaza]].<ref name="bloomberg_20191103_200arrested" /><ref name="asiatimes_20191104_knifeattack" /> Meanwhile, pro-Beijing lawmaker Junius Ho was stabbed and his parent's grave was desecrated.<ref name="bbc_50312310" /><ref name="scmp_3019750" />

[[File:Yuen Long Station White Tee people attack citizen in platform 20190721.png|thumb|left|White-clad men assaulted commuters and protesters with sticks inside [[Yuen Long station]] on 21&nbsp;July&nbsp;2019.]]

The [[2019 Yuen Long attack]] occurred following a mass protest organised by the CHRF on 21&nbsp;July. Suspected gangsters vowed that they would "defend" their "homeland" and warned all anti-extradition bill protesters not to set foot in Yuen Long.<ref name="rthk_1469620" /> Perpetrators were indiscriminately attacking commuters in the concourse and on the platform of Yuen Long station, as well as inside train compartments, resulting in a widespread backlash from the community. The Department of Justice has since been criticised by some lawyers for making "politically motivated" prosecutions. After the Yuen Long attack, no assailant was charged for weeks after the event, while young protesters were charged with rioting within several days.<ref name="scmp_3021860" /> Protesters were also attacked with [[fireworks]] in Tin Shui Wai on 31&nbsp;July,<ref name="hkfp_20190731_fireworkattack" /> and then attacked by knife-wielding men in Tsuen Wan<ref name="hkfp_20190806_mibslashed" /> and suspected "Fujianese" gang members wielding long poles in North Point on 5&nbsp;August, though they fought back against the attackers.<ref name="nyt_20190811_subwaygas" /><ref name="guardian_20190805_riotpolice" />

{{external media| float = right| width = 230px| video1 = [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=cNyKZWYCqpU The 31 July 2019 incident in which protesters were attacked by fireworks launching out of a moving vehicle] (BBC News)| video2 = [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=oKXVqsyMEdw The 11 November 2019 incident in which a man was set on fire by a protester] (Bloomberg)}}

Amidst frustration that police had failed to prosecute pro-government violent counter-protesters and being increasingly distrustful of police because of this,<ref name="nyt_20191007_ownjustice" /> hard-core protesters began to carry out [[Vigilantism|vigilante attacks]]—described by protesters as "settling matters privately" —targeting individuals perceived to be foes.<ref name="nyt_20191007_ownjustice" /><ref name="telegraph_20191008_vigilanteviolence" /> Pro-Beijing actress [[Celine Ma]],<ref name="malaymail_1798017_celinema" /> plainclothed officers,<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/politics/article/3046726/hong-kong-protests-tear-gas-fired-arrests-rally-universal|title=Hong Kong protests: two plain-clothes police officers beaten up, tear gas fired and rally organiser arrested as mayhem breaks out in Central|first=Phila|last=Siu|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|date=January 19, 2020|access-date=October 7, 2020}}</ref> and a taxi driver who drove into a crowd of protesters in [[Sham Shui Po]] on 8&nbsp;October, were attacked.<ref name="scmp_3033050" /> A middle-aged man was doused with flammable liquid and set on fire by a protester after he had an altercation with protesters at [[Ma On Shan station]] on 11&nbsp;November.<ref name="fr24_20191113_manonfire" /><ref name="hkfp_20191111_manonfire" /> On 14&nbsp;November, an elderly man died from head injuries sustained earlier during a violent confrontation between two groups of protesters and pro-government Sheung Shui residents.<ref name="ch-lc" />

==Deaths==
=== By suicide ===
[[File:Man protesting Hong Kong's extradition law in Pacific Place 20190615.png|thumb|left|Marco Leung Ling-kit on scaffolding at [[Pacific Place (Hong Kong)|Pacific Place]] before he fell to his death on 15 June.]]
[[File:Hong Kong protests suicides mourning area.jpg|thumb|Mourning area for Marco Leung and Lo, located near [[Central Government Complex (Hong Kong)|Central Government Complex]], which was later destroyed by a pro-government group.]]
Several suicide cases were linked to the protests. The first suicide took place on 15&nbsp;June 2019, when 35-year-old Marco Leung Ling-kit climbed the elevated podium on the rooftop of [[Pacific Place (Hong Kong)|Pacific Place]] in Admiralty, and hung banners on the scaffolding with several anti-extradition slogans.<ref name="time_5607742" /><ref name="straitstimes_20190616_fatalfall" /> He wore a yellow raincoat with the words "Carrie Lam is killing Hong Kong" in Chinese written on the back.<ref name="time_5607742" /> After a five-hour standoff, during which police officers and Democratic Party legislator [[Roy Kwong]] attempted to talk him down, Leung fell to his death, missing an inflatable cushion set up by firefighters.<ref name="hkfp_20190615_manjump" /><ref name="time_5607742" /><ref name="straitstimes_20190616_fatalfall" /> His anti-extradition slogans later became the foundations for the "five demands" of the protests.<ref name="slogan explainer" /> A ''[[The Guardian|Guardian]]'' article dated 22 October 2019 reported that "protesters have tracked at least nine cases of suicides that appear to be linked directly to the demonstrations" since June.<ref name="guardian_20191022_mentalhealth" /> In five of these cases, the victims left a [[suicide note]] referring to the protests, and three were attributed to events following the extradition bill.<ref name="straitstimes_20190616_fatalfall" /><ref name="appledaily_60010806" /><ref name="oncc_20190629" /><ref name="shanghaiist_20190701_thirdsuicide" />

Several deaths, most notably, that of [[death of Chan Yin-lam|Chan Yin-lam]], a 15-year-old girl whom the police suspected had committed suicide, were the subject of a [[conspiracy theory]] given the unusual circumstances surrounding her death. Chan's naked corpse was found floating in the sea near Yau Tong on 22 September 2019, despite her having been an avid swimmer.<ref name="GeMyK" /> Protesters alleged that the police murdered them for participating in the protests and covered-up the deaths.<ref name="eji_20191028_fakenews" /><ref name="bloomberg_20191111_fakenews" /><ref name="trust vacuum" /> The police, in response, denied those allegations, and were supported in this by a March 2020 report by the [[United States Department of State]].<ref name="PWGgh" />

===During confrontations===
{{Main|Death of Chow Tsz-lok|Death of Luo Changqing}}
[[File:DSCF1540 (49037258931).jpg|thumb|right|Shrine at the site where <br />Chow Tsz-lok fell.]]
On 8&nbsp;November 2019, university student [[Death of Chow Tsz-lok|Chow Tsz-Lok died]] from severe head injuries sustained early on 4&nbsp;November after falling from the third floor onto the second story of a car park in Tseung Kwan O. This was close to an area where authorities were dispersing protesters.<ref name="scmp_3037000" /><ref name="cnn_20191107_studentfall" /> The cause of his fall remains unknown.<ref name="hkfp_20191106_denyblocking" /> Protesters and the police accused each other of obstructing ambulance access to Chow delaying his treatment.<ref name="hkfp_20191106_denyblocking" /><ref name="cnn_20191107_studentfall" /> The [[Hong Kong Fire Services Department|Fire Services Department]] stated that the ambulance assigned to Chow was blocked by buses and private vehicles and it had not come in contact with the police who were on duty.<ref name="nyt_20191107_studentdies" /> Chow's death was the first fatality linked to a scene where police officers and protesters clashed.<ref name="nyt_20191107_studentdies" /> The incident has been the subject of claims, widely spread online, about police responsibility for Chow's death.<ref name="bloomberg_20191111_fakenews" /><ref>{{cite web |title=Report: Alex Chow |url=https://www.osinthk.org/2019/12/05/alex-chow-findings/ |access-date=3 September 2020}}</ref> Protesters and human rights organisations demanded an independent investigation of the incident.<ref name="20191108amnesty" /><ref name="nyt-aihk" />

{{external media| float = right| width = 230px| video1 = [https://twitter.com/SCMPHongKong/status/1195266346914742274 The 13 November 2019 Sheung Shui clash, including the fatal throw] (SCMP)}}
On 14&nbsp;November, Luo Changqing,<ref name="ch-lc" /> a 70-year-old man, [[Killing of Luo Changqing|died]] from head injuries sustained the previous day when he was reportedly hit in the head by a brick thrown at him by a protester.<ref name="st-hpcd" /> On the day before in Sheung Shui, a violent clash erupted between a group of anti-government protesters and pro-government residents, during which both groups hurled bricks at each other.<ref name="scmp_3037601" /><ref name="cnn_20191114_elderlydies" /> The man was a bystander recording protestors during the conflict using his mobile phone<ref name="straitstimes_20191115_classifydeath" /> and was one of the locals who were clearing the bricks earlier.<ref name="scmp_3037601" /> His death is the first fatality directly attributed to the protests.<ref name="smh_p53d3v" /> Five people were arrested for murder.<ref name="rthk_1497614" />

==Tactics and methods==
{{main|Tactics and methods surrounding the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests}}
[[File:Tai Po Market Station underground tunnel Lennon Wall 20190709.jpg|thumb|A subway near [[Tai Po Market station]], dubbed the "Lennon Tunnel"]]
[[File:Flag of Hong Kong (Black Bauhinia with wilted petals variant).svg|thumb|[[Black Bauhinia Flag]], a variation of the [[flag of Hong Kong]]]]
[[File:Give Me Liberty - Hong Kong Graffiti 6 October 2019 (48851461613).jpg|thumb|right|[[Pepe the Frog]] became a symbol of resistance during the protests. "Give me Liberty or Give me Death!" alludes to [[Patrick Henry]]'s speech in support of the American Revolution.]]
[[File:IMGP1850 (49151831526).jpg|thumb|right|A [[Winnie-the-Pooh|Winnie the Pooh]] toy used to symbolise [[Xi Jinping]] with the [[Art of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests#Flags and symbols|Chinazi]] flag stuck on it, used in the 1&nbsp;December 2019 protests]]

The protests have been described as being largely "leaderless".<ref name="bloomberg_20190823_onlineworld" /> No group or political party has claimed leadership over the movement, though civic groups and prominent politicians have played a supportive role, such as applying for Letters of No Objection from the police or mediating conflicts between protesters and police officers.<ref name="latimes_20190614_withoutleaders" /> Protesters commonly used [[LIHKG]], an online forum similar to [[Reddit]], as well as [[Telegram (software)|Telegram]], an [[Comparison of instant messaging clients#Messengers with client-to-client encryption|optionally end-to-end encrypted messaging service]] to communicate and brainstorm ideas for protests and to make collective decisions.<ref name="bloomberg_20190823_onlineworld" /> Unlike previous protests, those of 2019 spread over 20 different neighbourhoods, so the entire territory witnessed them.<ref name="cna_11783460" /> Protesters and their supporters remained anonymous to avoid prosecutions or future potential retaliation from the authorities, employers who had a different political orientation, and corporations which kowtowed to political pressure.<ref name="theverge20926585" />

For the most part there are two groups of protesters, namely the "peaceful, rational and non-violent" protesters and the "fighters" group.<ref name="guardian_20190818_fightresist" /> Nonetheless, despite differences in methods, both groups have refrained from denouncing or criticising the other and provided tacit support. The principle was the "Do Not Split"— [[Praxis (process)|praxis]]—which was aimed to promote mutual respect for different views within the same protest movement. This was a response to the failure of the Umbrella Revolution which fell apart partly due to internal conflicts within the pro-democratic bloc.<ref name="nyt_20190628_selfregulate" /><ref name="caught fire" />

===Moderate group===
{{further|Art of the 2019–20 Hong Kong protests}}

The moderate group participated in different capacities.
