[[File:Nathan Law speaks at the US Capitol.jpg|thumb|Activists including [[Joshua Wong]] and [[Nathan Law]] met [[United States House of Representatives|House]] [[Democratic Party (United States)|Democratic]] leader [[Nancy Pelosi]] and Representative [[Chris Smith (New Jersey politician)|Chris Smith]] at the US Congress.|alt=]]

The pro-Beijing camp supported the government in promoting the bill, though [[flip-flop (politics)|U-turned]] when the government withdrew the bill.<ref name="eji_20190620" /> They condemned the use of violence by protesters, including breaking into the LegCo Complex and using petrol bombs and unidentified liquids against the police,<ref name="rthk_1466047" /><ref name="standardhk_211229_violentactions" /> and used the term "rubbish youths" ({{zh|links=no|c=廢青}}) to refer to high school- and university-age participants.<ref name="scmp_1866535" /> They maintained their support for the Hong Kong Police Force and held various counter-demonstrations to support them,<ref name="rthk_1468821" /><ref name="rthk_1468634" /><ref name="inmediahk_1065744" /> and criticised the government for not taking enough actions to "halt the violence".<ref name="jWuw5" /> Members of the [[Executive Council of Hong Kong|Executive Council]], [[Ip Kwok-him]] and [[Regina Ip]] alleged that there was a "mastermind" behind the protests but could not provide substantial evidence to support their claim.<ref name="eji_20190809" />

Many lawmakers from the pan-democratic camp, such as Ted Hui and Roy Kwong, assisted the protesters in various scenarios.<ref name="time_5628021" /> Responding to the escalation of the mid-August protests at the airport, the convenor of the pro-democratic caucas, [[Claudia Mo]], while disagreeing with some protesters' actions, asserted that her group of lawmakers would not split with the protesters.<ref name="rfa_20190814_airportreopens" /><ref name="hk01_363679_protestersaware" /><ref name="standnews_20190715_roadblock" /> Pro-democrats also condemned the arrests of and the violence directed at the protests' organisers, lawmakers and election candidates.<ref name="independent_9183101" /> Former government executives, including [[Anson Chan]], the former [[Chief Secretary for Administration]], issued several open letters to Carrie Lam, urging her to respond to the five core demands raised by protesters.<ref name="hkfp_20190724_secondappeal" />

In August, 17 members from the [[Real Estate Developers Association of Hong Kong]] and the [[Chinese General Chamber of Commerce]] released statements condemning the escalating protests because of the instability they had brought to the city's economy and business community, as well as the negative effects on society as a whole.<ref name="standardhk_210500" /> [[Annie Wu (businesswoman)|Annie Wu]], the daughter of Maxim's founder and also a member of the [[Chinese People's Political Consultative Conference]], condemned the protesters at the [[United Nations Human Rights Council]] and suggested that Hong Kong should give up the "lost" protesters.<ref name="thestandard213241" /><ref name="5Lh0c" /> On 30&nbsp;October, [[Abraham Shek]], a lawmaker representing the [[Real Estate and Construction (constituency)|Real Estate and Construction constituency]], supported the formation of an independent commission and said that the problem could not be resolved by addressing the severe housing shortage.<ref name="hkfp_20191030_independentprobe" /> Tycoon [[Li Ka-shing]] took out a two-page advertisement in newspapers, urging people to "stop anger and violence in the name of love", and quoting a Chinese poem: "The melon of Huangtai cannot bear the picking again".<ref name="scmp_3023037" />

Despite the government, the pro-Beijing camp and state media invoking the notion of a "[[silent majority]]" who opposed the protests, and urging citizens to cut ties with the "violent protesters", citizens generally favoured the pro-democratic camp and supported the protest movement.<ref name="20191126newstatesman" /> The 2019 Hong Kong District Council election, the first poll since the beginning of the protests, had been billed as a "referendum" on the government.<ref name="nbc_1090206" /> More than 2.94&nbsp;million votes were cast for a turnout rate of 71.2%, up from 1.45&nbsp;million and 47% from the previous election.<ref name="aljazeera_191124051609941" /> This was the highest turnout in Hong Kong's history, both in absolute numbers and in turnout rates.<ref name="euronews_20191124_electionpolls" /> The [[Results breakdown of the 2019 Hong Kong local elections|results]] were a resounding [[landslide victory]] for the pro-democracy bloc, as they saw their seat share increased from 30% to almost 88%, with a jump in vote share from 40% to 57%.<ref name="euronews_20191124_electionpolls" /> Among those who were also legislators, the overwhelming majority of the losing candidates were from the pro-Beijing bloc.<ref name="rthk_1494247" />

[[Reuters]] conducted polls in December 2019,<ref>{{Cite news|first1=James|last1=Pomfret|first2=Clare|last2=Jim|date=2019-12-31|title=Exclusive: Hong Kongers support protester demands; minority wants independence from China - Reuters poll|language=en|work=Reuters|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-hongkong-protests-poll-exclusive-idUSKBN1YZ0VK|access-date=2020-09-20}}</ref> March 2020,<ref>{{Cite news|author=Reuters Staff|date=2020-03-27|title=Factbox: What Hong Kong opinion poll respondents are saying about protests|language=en|work=Reuters|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-hongkong-protests-poll-factbox-idUSKBN21E18O|access-date=2020-09-20}}</ref> June 2020<ref name="kF9Vz" /> and August 2020. The last poll showed  that an increasing number of [[Hongkongers]] support the pro-democracy goals since the [[Hong Kong national security law|national security law]] was implemented. More than half of the respondents opposed the national security law. 70% wants an independent commission of inquiry that looks into how the police handled the protests. 63% wants universal suffrage. The support for amnesty of all arrested protesters rose to 50%. More than half of people still wants [[Carrie Lam]] to resign. The number of people who oppose the pro-democracy demands went down to 19%. The majority still opposes Hong Kong independence which is 60%, 20% supports the idea.<ref>{{Cite news|author=Reuters Staff|date=2020-08-30|title=Exclusive: HK survey shows increasing majority back pro-democracy goals, smaller support for protest movement|language=en|work=Reuters|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-hongkong-security-poll-exclusive-idUSKBN25Q00U|access-date=2020-09-20}}</ref>

===Mainland China reactions===
{{further|National People's Congress decision on Hong Kong national security legislation}}

The Chinese government expressed their opposition to the protests, while taking measures against the protests and their supporters. The protests were depicted by the government and media as separatist riots.<ref name="reuters_20190610_foreignforces" /> Beijing accused the movement of displaying "characteristics of [[colour revolution]]s" and "signs of terrorism".<ref name="scmp_3021877" /><ref name="eji_20190813_beijing" /> The Beijing government and state-run media accused foreign forces of interfering with domestic affairs and supporting the protesters.<ref name="hkfp_20190910_joushuagerman" /> These allegations were rejected by Hong Kong pro-democrats,<ref name="hkfp_20190808_colourrevolution" /> and [[CNN]] noted that China had a record of blaming foreign forces for causing domestic unrest.<ref name="cnn_20190731_usblamed" /> On 22&nbsp;October 2019, following protests and violence in [[2019 Catalan protests|Catalonia]] and [[2019 Chilean protests|Chile]], the Chinese government accused Western media of hypocrisy for not providing similar coverage and support to those protests.<ref name="cnn_20191021_chilespain" /><ref name="cna_12025908" /> Chinese diplomats and ambassadors in more than 70 countries broadcast Beijing's position on the protests to shape international opinion.<ref name="scmp3026465" /> General Secretary Xi Jinping and [[Premier of the People's Republic of China|Premier]] [[Li Keqiang]] have repeatedly backed [[Carrie Lam as Chief Executive of Hong Kong|Lam's administration]] and the police.<ref name="sau2J" />

Chinese state media outlets largely ignored the protests until 17&nbsp;April 2019.<ref name="hkfp_20190716_chinapropaganda" /> The protests were mostly [[Internet censorship in China|censored from Mainland Chinese]] social media, such as [[Sina Weibo]], though state-owned media and Chinese social media users later condemned the protesters.<ref name="ft_20190610_newscensored" /> State-run media pressured various companies, including railway operator MTR Corporation and airline [[Cathay Pacific]] to take a hardline approach against employees who took part in the protests. Cathay Pacific saw its top managers "reshuffled" and began firing pro-democratic employees after the [[Civil Aviation Administration of China]] threatened to block Cathay's access to Chinese airspace,<ref name="scmp_3022284" /> while the MTR began to close stations and end its service early after being criticised for transporting protesters.<ref name="scmp_3027064" /><ref name="cnbc_20191008" /><ref name="hollywood_1245783" /> Chinese media also attempted to appeal to the "silent majority"<ref name="20190805straitstimes" /> and blame the protests on [[Education in Hong Kong|Hong Kong's education system]].<ref name="20190924telegraph" /> It also hailed police officers as "heroes",<ref name="scmp3021987" /> and demanded the government take more "forceful" actions and the court to hand out heavy punishments,<ref name="scmp3031814" /><ref name="scmp3020454" />

Foreign envoys reported the deployment in late August of a sizeable number of [[People's Liberation Army]] (PLA) troops to Hong Kong, well beyond the usual rotation and possibly doubling the number of PLA troops than before the start of the protests. Drills by the [[People's Armed Police]] were observed across the border in Shenzhen in August.<ref name="reuters_20190930_doublestroop" /> On 6&nbsp;October 2019, the PLA issued its first warning to the protesters, who were shining laser lights on the exterior of the PLA garrison in [[Kowloon Tong]].<ref name="reuters_20191006_laserbarracks" /> On 16 November, soldiers appeared publicly in the streets for the first time during the protests, in plain clothes and unarmed, to clear roadblocks and other debris left during protests alongside local residents, firefighters, and police officers before marching back to the Kowloon Tong barracks. The government insisted the soldiers were volunteers, and that it had made no request for assistance.<ref name="scmp_3038049" /> The act was criticised by pro-democrats who deemed it a violation of the Basic Law.<ref name="rthk_1492648" /> The Chinese government required goods mailed from mainland China to Hong Kong to be investigated while goods which were believed to relate to the protests were blocked.<ref name="scmp_3033312" /><ref name="reuters_20191018_blackclothing" /> Chinese authorities also detained several individuals in mainland China after they voiced their support for the protesters.<ref name="CLfbQ" />

China further tightened its control in Hong Kong in 2020: on 4&nbsp;January, the [[State Council of the People's Republic of China|State Council]] dismissed [[Wang Zhimin]] from the role of director of the Hong Kong Liaison Office and appointed [[Luo Huining]] as his successor. The decision was widely linked to the poor performance of pro-government candidates at the District Council elections in November, and Wang's perceived poor judgment of how the protests evolved.<ref name="scmp_3044684" /><ref name="nyt_20190104_replacement" /> [[Hong Kong and Macau Affairs Office]] director [[Zhang Xiaoming]] was demoted and replaced by [[Xia Baolong]] in February 2020.<ref name="9KjjJ" /> The new directors triggered the [[Hong Kong Basic Law#Controversy|Basic Law Article 22 controversy]] in April when they claimed that the two offices were not covered by Article&nbsp;22.<ref name="nC4Je" /> In May, China announced that the NPCSC, China's [[Rubber stamp (politics)|rubber-stamp]] legislative body, would directly draft a national security law for Hong Kong and skip the local legislation procedures.<ref name="l2hYc" /> Political analysts believed that Beijing's action would mark the end of the "[[one country, two systems]]" principle and Hong Kong's autonomy as promised in the [[Sino-British Joint Declaration]].<ref name="Z19zk" /><ref name="eDi1P" /> On 28 May 2020, the NPC approved the controversial national security laws for Hong Kong. The legislation allows the government's national security agencies to operate in Hong Kong.<ref name="ODHRD" /> On 30 June 2020, China implemented "Hong Kong national security law". Its 66 articles target crimes of secession, subversion, terrorism and collusion with foreign forces, and includes serious penalties between 10 years of prison to life [[imprisonment]].<ref name="i6D2y" />

===International reactions===
{{further|Responses to the 2020 Chinese involvement with Hong Kong national security law|Overseas censorship of Chinese issues#Censored topics}}

As a result of the protests, many nations issued [[travel warning]]s for Hong Kong.<ref name="standardhk_50006293" /> Demonstrations in reaction to the extradition protests also took place in various locations around the world, including: Australia,<ref name="guardian_20190724" /> Brazil, Canada,<ref name="rthk_1472801" /> Chile, France, Germany,<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.tagesspiegel.de/politik/vorfaelle-in-hamburg-china-geht-auch-in-deutschland-gegen-demokratieaktivisten-aus-hongkong-vor/25011814.html|title=China geht auch in Deutschland gegen Demokratieaktivisten aus Hongkong vor|work=[[tagesspiegel.de]]|date=13 September 2019|access-date=31 July 2020|language=de}}</ref> India, Italy, Japan, Lithuania,<ref name="lrt_1090902" /> South Africa, South Korea,<ref name="ltn_2977410" /> Taiwan, the United Kingdom, the United States and Vietnam.<ref name="scmp_3013748" /><ref>{{cite web|url=https://hongkongfp.com/2019/09/30/pictures-40-cities-hold-anti-totalitarianism-rallies-solidarity-hong-kong-protest-movement/|title=In Pictures: Over 40 cities hold anti-totalitarianism rallies in solidarity with Hong Kong protest movement|first=Holmes|last=Chan|work=[[Hong Kong Free Press]]|date=30 September 2019|access-date=31 July 2020}}</ref> Solidarity rallies held by Hong Kong international students studying abroad were often disrupted by mainland Chinese supporters.<ref name="scmp_3019888" /><ref name="guardian_20190724" /><ref name="scmp_3030548" /><ref name="bbc_49914304" /> Following the death of Chow Tsz-lok, Secretary for Justice Teresa Cheng was heckled and jostled by protest supporters in [[Bloomsbury Square]] in London; she fell to the ground and injured her arm.<ref name="hkfp_20191115_justicesecretary" /> Some protesters in the concurrent 2019 Catalan protests have claimed inspiration from, and solidarity with the Hong Kong protests.<ref name="hkfp_20191025_catalanparallel" /><ref name="straitstimes_20191020_catalaninspired" /> Protesters also formed the [[Milk Tea Alliance]] with Taiwanese and Thai netizens to counter online mainland Chinese trolls, but it slowly evolves into an online democratic solidarity movement that advocates for democracy in Southeast Asia.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://qz.com/1919253/what-is-the-milk-tea-alliance/|title=Thailand and Hong Kong are brewing an anti-authoritarian #MilkTeaAlliance|first=Mary|last=Hui|work=[[Quartz (publication)|Quartz]]|date=October 19, 2020|access-date=October 20, 2020}}</ref>

[[File:Secretary of State Michael R. Pompeo remarks to the Press, at the Department of State DOD 107449649-5dd33804c3e81.webm|thumb|start=07:17|US Secretary of State [[Mike Pompeo]] comments on 18&nbsp;November&nbsp;2019.]]

Some protesters fled to Taiwan to avoid prosecution.<ref name="rthk-exiled" /> The Hong Kong protests were considered a contributing factor in the landslide victory of [[Tsai Ing-wen]] during the [[2020 Taiwanese presidential election]]. Tsai, who had repeatedly shown a supportive attitude toward the Hong Kong protesters, used the slogan "today Hong Kong, tomorrow Taiwan" during her presidential campaign, referring to the city's unrest as evidence of the threats posed by the "one country, two systems" principle to Taiwan's autonomy and democracy.<ref name="ft-taiwan-election" /> Christina Lai from [[Academia Sinica]] concurred that the situation in Hong Kong created a sense of "urgency" for Taiwanese voters, as China's hardline reaction implied that they would use the same strategy to undermine Taiwan's autonomy in the future. Tsai's rejection of the principle enabled her to gain support from young voters.<ref name="ML47Z" />

In the United States, the House of Representatives and [[United States Senate|Senate]] both unanimously passed the [[Hong Kong Human Rights and Democracy Act]] in light of the extradition bill and protests.<ref name="reuters_20191114_quickpassage" /><ref name="bloomberg_20191119_senatebill" /><ref name="wsj_11574205044" /><ref name="reuters_20191119_senatebill" /> President [[Donald Trump]] signed the bill on 27&nbsp;November, alongside a companion bill restricting US exports of crowd control devices to the Hong Kong police forces.<ref name="cnbc_20191127_trumpbill" /> Various US politicians have expressed disapproval of corporate decisions related to the protests.<ref name="bi_20191008_nbaoffense" /><ref name="hill_464748" /><ref name="vox_20808691" /><ref name="time_5653973" /> On 29 May 2020, Trump ordered the removal of the special status enjoyed by Hong Kong due to Beijing's new national security law for the territory, after Pompeo declared that the city was no longer autonomous from China and should therefore, be treated as any one of Chinese cities.<ref name="YV3hH" />

[[Dominic Raab]], the [[Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs|Foreign Secretary]] of the United Kingdom, urged China to uphold the promises it made in the [[Sino-British Joint Declaration]], which was a legally binding international treaty.<ref name="1t0E6" /> The UK had already stopped selling crowd control equipment to the HKPF.<ref name="BWB8i" /> Former UK consulate employee [[Simon Cheng]] was granted asylum in the UK in June 2020. He was previously detained by Chinese authorities who reportedly tortured him to force a confession that the UK was involved in instigating the protests, though Chinese authorities stated that he was detained for "soliciting prostitutes".<ref name="4Zo55" /> On 3 June 2020, Prime Minister [[Boris Johnson]] announced that if China were to continue pursuing the national security law, he would open a path to British citizenship for Hong Kong residents who were eligible for a [[British National (Overseas)]] passport (BNO).<ref name="603Zg" /> After the passing of the law on 30 June 2020, the UK confirmed these Hong Kong residents are able to come to the United Kingdom with a five-year limited leave to remain.<ref name="HMaXR" /> Following those five years they will be able to apply for [[indefinite leave to remain|indefinite leave to remain in the United Kingdom]] and, after a further 12 months with settled status, they will be able to apply for [[British nationality law|British citizenship]].<ref name="fZJkH" />

[[United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights]] [[Michelle Bachelet]] demanded the Hong Kong government conduct an investigation into police use of force against the protesters;<ref>{{cite news |title=China accuses UN human rights chief of inflaming Hong Kong unrest |url=https://www.dw.com/en/china-accuses-un-human-rights-chief-of-inflaming-hong-kong-unrest/a-51486730 |access-date=29 November 2020}}</ref> she previously said that she was "troubled and alarmed" by the escalating violence used by the protesters.<ref name="hkfp_20191006_unchief" /> Amnesty International praised the protesters for their dedication despite facing "abusive policing tactics" which include the "wanton use of tear gas, arbitrary arrests, physical assaults and abuses in detention".<ref name="scmp3048110" /> [[Kenneth Roth]], the head of [[Human Rights Watch]] (HRW), was denied entry to Hong Kong at Hong Kong International Airport on 12&nbsp;January 2020. Hong Kong officials insisted that the decision to bar Roth from entry had been made in Hong Kong, not in mainland China.<ref name="hkfp_20200114_hrwdeniedentry" /> In June 2020, on the first anniversary of mass protests in Hong Kong, a statement released by HRW said that the governments of both China and Hong Kong should respect fundamental rights of people.<ref name="wnInG" />

Norwegian lawmaker [[Guri Melby]],<ref name="EKfkc" /> and US members of Congress [[Marco Rubio]] and [[Jim McGovern (American politician)|James McGovern]] announced their intention to nominate the Hong Kong protesters for the Nobel Peace Prize in October 2019 and February 2020 respectively.<ref name="20200205bloomberg" />

== In popular culture ==
* "The Battle for Hong Kong", by [[Channel 4]], has a look into the lives of some of the protesters.<ref name="MuFnt" />
* The second episode of "China: A New World Order", by the [[BBC]], has the protests as central theme.<ref name="NSBJx" />
